Definition, Proofs, and Sunan of Tayammum
Definition, proofs, sunan, and ikhtilaf of tayammum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of tayammum, cite its proofs from Quran and Sunnah, list its eight sunan, and explain the intra-Hanafi difference regarding badaliyyah.

Core Idea
Tayammum is a substitute purification with earth, proven by Quran, Sunnah, and consensus.

Tayammum is a special dispensation for purification when water is unavailable. This lesson unpacks its definition, its primary textual proofs, the eight recommended acts (sunan), and a subtle yet consequential difference among the early Hanafi masters regarding the nature of substitution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic and legal definitions
Break down the author's definition: linguistically 'intention', legally 'intending pure earth for purification'. Note the clarification that tayammum is the wiping of face and hands with intention, not merely intending earth.
This distinction matters for validity: intention (niyyah) is a condition, not part of the defined action.
2
Trace the proofs (dalil)
Locate the three proofs: Quran (4:43 imperative 'fatayammamu'), Sunnah (hadith of earth as mosque and purifier), and Ijma' (consensus). Understand how the imperative indicates obligation in Hanafi usul.
The hadith is from Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim; it establishes the permissibility and purifying nature of earth.
3
List the eight sunan
From the text, enumerate the eight sunan: striking with inner palms, turning them forward and backward, shaking, spreading fingers, saying Bismillah, sequence (tartib), and continuity (muwalat).
These are recommended, not obligatory; omitting them does not invalidate tayammum.
4
Understand the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on badaliyyah
Note the difference: according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, substitution is between water and earth (element); according to Imam Muhammad, it is between the actions of tayammum and wudu (act).
This affects whether a person with wudu can pray behind an imam with tayammum.
5
Apply the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): substitution is between water and earth. Thus, following an imam with tayammum is valid for one with wudu.
Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah prefers this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب التيمم: التيمم لغة: القصد، وشرعاً: عبارة عن القصد إلى الصعيد للتطهير، وثبوته بالكتاب، وهو قوله تعالى: ﴿فَتَيَمَّمُوا صَعِيداً طَيِّباً (النساء:٤٣)، وبالسنة، وهو قوله عليه: جعلت لي الأرض مسجداً وطهوراً، أينما أدركتني الصلاة تيممت وصليت"، وقد انعقد الإجماع على ثبوته، ولم يكن مشروعاً لغير هذه الأمة، وسننه لمانية: الضرب بباطن كفيه وإقبالهما، وإدبارهما، ونفضهما، وتفريج أصابعه والتسمية، والترتيب، والولاء، كذا في "المستخلص" و"الطائي" وقال في "فتح المعين": شرع في غزوة المريسيع لما أضلت عائشة ما عقدها في شعبان سنة ست من الهجرة والرخصة لنا فيه من حيث الآلة حيث اكتفى فيه بالصعيد الذي هو ملوث، ومن حيث المحل؛ للاختصار فيه على شطر الأعضاء، وما قيل في تعريفه القصد إلى الصعيد الطاهر لإزالة الحدث، ففيه مسامحة؛ لأن القصد شرطه، فهو اسم لمسح الوجه واليدين بشرط النية، والمناسبة بينه وبين الوضوء أن الوضوء أصل والتيمم خلفه، ولهذا أخره، لكن البدلية عندهما بين الماء والتراب وعند محمد بين الفعلين، يعني التيمم والوضوء، ويتفرع عليه جواز اقتداء المتوضئ بالتيمم عندهما لا عنده.
Chapter of Tayammum: Tayammum linguistically means intention. Legally: it is the expression for intending pure earth for purification. Its establishment is by the Book: His statement the Exalted: "And seek pure earth" (An-Nisa:43). And by the Sunnah: His statement (peace be upon him): "The earth has been made for me a mosque and a purifier. Wherever the prayer overtakes me, I perform tayammum and pray." And consensus has been reached on its establishment. It was not legislated for any other nation. Its sunan are eight: striking with the inner palms and turning them forward and backward, shaking them, spreading the fingers, saying the basmalah, sequence, and continuity. Thus in 'Al-Mustakhlas' and 'Al-Ta'i'. And it is said in 'Fath al-Mu'in': It was legislated in the Ghazwah of al-Muraysi' when 'A'ishah lost her necklace in Sha'ban of the sixth year of the Hijrah. The permission for us in it is from the aspect of the means, as pure earth which is dirty suffices, and from the aspect of the body parts, as it is limited to half the limbs. What is said in its definition: 'intending pure earth to remove ritual impurity' contains imprecision, because intention is a condition. So it is the name for wiping the face and hands with the condition of intention. The relationship between it and wudu is that wudu is the original and tayammum is its substitute, thus it was mentioned later. However, according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), substitution is between water and earth, while according to Muhammad (al-Shaybani) it is between the two actions, meaning tayammum and wudu. Derived from this is the permissibility of the person with wudu following the one with tayammum in prayer according to them, not according to him.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage defines tayammum both linguistically and legally, then provides its textual proofs from the Quran (4:43) and the Sunnah (hadith of the earth as a mosque and purifier), and notes that consensus (ijma') has affirmed it. It also states that tayammum was a special dispensation for the Muslim nation alone. The passage then lists the eight sunan (recommended acts) of tayammum: striking the inner palms, turning them forward and backward, shaking them, spreading the fingers, saying Bismillah, performing the actions in sequence (tartib), and maintaining continuity (muwalat). It also clarifies the precise legal definition: tayammum is the wiping of the face and hands with intention, not merely intending earth. Finally, it discusses a subtle technical difference among the early Hanafi masters: according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the substitution (badaliyyah) is between water and pure earth, whereas according to Imam Muhammad (in another opinion), it is between the two actions of tayammum and wudu. This difference has a practical consequence: if a person with wudu prays behind an imam performing tayammum, the prayer is valid according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), but not according to Imam Muhammad's view.

Key Terms
التيمم (al-tayammum): Linguistically: intention. Legally: wiping the face and hands with pure earth with intention as a substitute for wudu.
الصعيد (al-sa'id): Pure earth or dust that can be used for tayammum.
البدلية (al-badaliyyah): The substitutive relationship between tayammum and wudu (or between earth and water).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many sunan does tayammum have?
A) Five
B) Six
C) Eight
D) Ten

Key Takeaway
Tayammum is a valid wajib purification substitute with earth, proven by Quran, Sunnah, and consensus, and its eight sunan should be observed, while the intra-Hanafi difference on badaliyyah affects the permissibility of following an imam with tayammum but the mufta bihi permits it.

8 min
Distance as an Excuse for Tayammum
Lists distance definitions and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi criteria for distance as an excuse for tayammum and apply Abu Yusuf's practical criterion to determine when water is considered distant.

Core Idea
Water is distant if fetching it makes you miss your caravan – a practical measure, not just fixed miles.

The Hanafi school permits tayammum when water is too far. But what does 'far' mean? The passage presents several definitions, from fixed mileage to the realistic test of the caravan disappearing. The strongest view, according to the author, is Abu Yusuf's practical criterion. This lesson unpacks those definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fixed measurements
Note that the base definition is one mile (a third of a farsakh, 4000 cubits). Some scholars extend to two miles.
One cubit = 24 fingers, roughly 0.5 m, so one mile ~2 km.
2
Understand the auditory criterion
Another opinion judges distance by whether one can hear the people at the water source.
This is less precise.
3
Analyze Abu Yusuf's criterion
He uses the caravan's departure: if going to fetch water would cause you to miss the caravan, water is distant.
This is subjective but realistic.
4
Recognize the preferred opinion
The author considers Abu Yusuf's view the best and closest to the truth.
It balances practicality and legal principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لبعده ميلاً: [هو ثلث الفرسخ أربعة آلاف ذراع، والذراع أربعة وعشرون إصبعاً بعد وحروف لا إله إلا الله محمد رسول الله (ع،ط)] سواء كان ذلك في المصر أو خارجها، وقيل في المسافر إذا كان الماء أمامه: يقدر بميلين، وعن محمد أنه يقدر بميلين مطلقاً، وقيل: إذا كان في موضع يسمع صوت أهل الماء فهو قريب وإلا فبعيد، وعن أبي يوسف أن الماء إذا كان بحيث لو ذهب إليه وتوضأ تذهب القافلة وتغيب عن البصر فهو بعيد، وإلا فقريب، وهو أحسن جداً، وما ذكره المصنف هو أقرب الأقوال. (عيني، مستخلص) لمرض: أي بأن خاف اشتداده باستعمال الماء، أو بالتحرك للاستعمال، أو لم يقدر على استعماله، وعند الشافعي: يتيمم إذا خاف تلف النفس أو العضو، وعندنا يتيمم مطلقاً، أما إذا لم يقدر المريض على الوضوء ولا التيمم وليس عنده من يوضئه أو ييممه فإنه لا يصلي عندهما، وفي الجامع الصغير": أن مقطوع اليدين والرجلين إن إنما كان بوجهه جراحة يصلي بغير طهارة ولا يتيمم ولا يعيد، وفي "التنوير" وشرحه فاقد الطهورين والتراب - لمرض يؤخرها، وقالا يتشبه بالمصلين، فيركع ويسجد إن وجد مكاناً يابساً، وإلا يؤمي يعيد، وبه يفتى، وإليه صح رجوع الإمام. (فتح المعين) أو برد: يعني إذا - الماء قائماً ثم خاف الجنب إن اغتسل أو توضاً أن يقتله البرد أو يمرضه يتيمم . مطلقاً، سواء كان خارج المصر أو فيه، وعندهما لا فيه؛ لأن يتيمم تيسر الماء الحار للمقيم في المصر ،غالب وللإمام أن العجز قد ثبت في حقه حقيقة فيعتبر، والأصح عدم جوازه للمحدث في المصر إجماعاً، والمراد بالخوف في المرض والبرد هو غلبة الظن عن أمارة أو تجربة أو بإخبار طبيب مسلم غير ظاهر الفسق. (فتح وغيره)
Due to his distance by a mile: it is one-third of a farsakh, four thousand cubits, and a cubit is twenty-four fingers. [Then some unclear letters: 'and the letters - there is no god but Allah, Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' (possibly a marginal note)]. Whether this is in the town or outside it. It is said regarding the traveler that if water is in front of him, it is measured as two miles. From Muhammad (al-Shaybani) that it is measured by two miles absolutely. It is said: if he is in a place where he can hear the people at the water, then it is near; otherwise it is far. From Abu Yusuf: if the water is such that if he went and performed wudu, the caravan would leave and disappear from sight, then it is far; otherwise it is near. This is very good. What the author (al-Mawsili) mentioned is the closest of the opinions. (al-'Ayni, al-Mustakhlas) Due to illness: meaning that he fears it will worsen by using water, or by moving to use it, or he is unable to use it. According to al-Shafi'i: he performs tayammum if he fears loss of life or limb. According to us, he performs tayammum absolutely. However, if the patient is unable to perform wudu nor tayammum and has no one to perform them for him, then he does not pray according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). In al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'The person whose hands and feet are cut off, if he has a wound on his face, he prays without purification, does not perform tayammum, and does not repeat.' In al-Tanwir and its commentary: 'One who lacks both water and earth due to an illness that delays them, the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: he imitates the praying people, so he bows and prostrates if he finds a dry place; otherwise he gestures and repeats. According to this fatwa is given, and to it the Imam (Abu Hanifa) is reported to have retracted. (Fath al-Mu'in) Or cold: meaning that if water is present, then the junub fears that if he bathes or performs wudu, the cold will kill him or make him ill, he performs tayammum absolutely, whether he is outside the town or inside it. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), not inside the town, because hot water is generally available for the resident in town. According to the Imam (Abu Hanifa), inability is established in his case truly, so it is considered. The correct view is that it is not permissible for the one with minor impurity (hadath) in the town by consensus. The meaning of 'fear' in illness and cold is preponderant belief based on a sign, experience, or the report of a Muslim doctor who is not apparently sinful. (Fath, etc.)
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage discusses three additional causes for tayammum: distance from water, illness, and severe cold, along with fear (khawf) of these. For distance, the text defines a mile as one-third farsakh (four thousand cubits), though there are varying opinions. The author states that the closest view is that of Abu Yusuf: water is 'distant' if going to it, performing wudu, and returning would cause one to miss the caravan or lose sight of it. For illness, Hanafis allow tayammum if water use would worsen the illness or the patient cannot move to use it. This is broader than al-Shafi'i, who only allows tayammum if life or limb is at risk. The text then discusses extreme cases: if a patient can neither perform wudu nor tayammum and has no one to assist, the two Sahibayn say do not pray, while the author prefers the view that one should imitate the movements of prayer without purification and repeat later. For cold (barad): a junub person who fears death or illness from cold water is allowed tayammum according to Abu Hanifa, even if in town, because his inability is genuine. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad do not allow it in town because hot water is generally available. The passage concludes with a definition of 'fear' (khawf): it must be based on overwhelming probability (ghalabat al-zann) from an observable sign, personal experience, or a trustworthy Muslim doctor's report. The text notes that for minor impurity (hadath) within the town, there is consensus that tayammum for cold is not allowed (because one can heat water or find a bathhouse).

Key Terms
المیل (al-mil): A measure of distance; in this context, approximately 4000 cubits (about 2 km), used to define when water is considered 'far'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the author prefer Abu Yusuf's criterion for distance?
A) Because it is based on the Quran.
B) Because it is practical and reflects the reality of hardship.
C) Because it is the strictest opinion.
D) Because it is the opinion of the majority of scholars.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prioritizes practical hardship over fixed measurements when defining distance for tayammum, as seen in Abu Yusuf's caravan criterion.

Illness as an Excuse for Tayammum
Discusses illness excuse and intra-Hanafi dispute on extreme inability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi conditions for tayammum due to illness, contrast them with the Shafi'i view, and state the ruling for a patient unable to perform any purification.

Core Idea
Hanafis allow tayammum for any worsening of illness, not just life-threatening danger.

Illness provides another excuse for tayammum. The Hanafi school takes a broad view: if using water would worsen the illness or the patient cannot move to use it, tayammum is allowed. This stands in contrast to the Shafi'i school, which restricts it to fear of death or limb loss. The passage also tackles an extreme case: what if the patient cannot purify at all? Here, there is an intra-Hanafi difference with the preferred view being to imitate prayer and repeat later.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi condition for illness
Fear that illness will worsen by using water, or inability to move to use water, permits tayammum.
This is broader than the Shafi'i restriction.
2
Compare with Shafi'i view
Al-Shafi'i requires fear of loss of life or limb; Hanafis require only fear of worsening.
Practical difference: a patient with a skin condition can use tayammum in Hanafi but not Shafi'i.
3
Examine the extreme case of complete inability
If a patient can neither do wudu nor tayammum and has no help, Sahibayn say do not pray; but the preferred view (Imam's retraction) is to imitate prayer and repeat later.
This applies to those missing hands/feet or having wounds.
4
Understand the legal principle at play
The fundamental principle is that necessity (hardship) expands the concession, but absolute inability does not remove the obligation entirely; instead, a substitute is performed.
Tayammum is itself a substitute for water; when both are impossible, the obligation is not dropped.

Severe Cold and Fear as Excuses for Tayammum
Discusses cold excuse and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on location

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on tayammum due to cold and apply the definition of 'fear' (ghalabat al-zann) in determining the excuse.

Core Idea
For cold, Imam Abu Hanifa allows tayammum even inside town for a junub; the two Sahibayn restrict it to outside town.

Severe cold is another excuse for tayammum. The key dispute within the Hanafi school is whether junub can perform tayammum inside a town where hot water may be available. Imam Abu Hanifa says yes, because the inability is real; the Sahibayn say no, because hot water can be obtained. The passage also defines the level of fear required: overwhelming probability (ghalabat al-zann) based on signs, experience, or a reliable doctor's report.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dispute on cold inside the town
For a junub who fears death or illness from cold water, Abu Hanifa permits tayammum even in town; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad do not allow it in town.
Their reasoning: hot water is generally available in towns.
2
Recognize the consensus on minor impurity in town
The text states that for a person with minor impurity (hadath), tayammum due to cold is not allowed in the town by consensus.
Because they can heat a small amount of water for wudu.
3
Define 'fear' (khawf) that justifies tayammum
Fear must be based on overwhelming probability (ghalabat al-zann) from a sign, experience, or report of a reliable Muslim doctor.
This standard applies to both illness and cold.
4
Apply the definition of fear to cold
A person who merely feels cold without strong indication cannot use this excuse; there must be a real risk.
The school is strict on the threshold to prevent abuse.

Commentary
This passage discusses three additional causes for tayammum: distance from water, illness, and severe cold, along with fear (khawf) of these. For distance, the text defines a mile as one-third farsakh (four thousand cubits), though there are varying opinions. The author states that the closest view is that of Abu Yusuf: water is 'distant' if going to it, performing wudu, and returning would cause one to miss the caravan or lose sight of it. For illness, Hanafis allow tayammum if water use would worsen the illness or the patient cannot move to use it. This is broader than al-Shafi'i, who only allows tayammum if life or limb is at risk. The text then discusses extreme cases: if a patient can neither perform wudu nor tayammum and has no one to assist, the two Sahibayn say do not pray, while the author prefers the view that one should imitate the movements of prayer without purification and repeat later. For cold (barad): a junub person who fears death or illness from cold water is allowed tayammum according to Abu Hanifa, even if in town, because his inability is genuine. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad do not allow it in town because hot water is generally available. The passage concludes with a definition of 'fear' (khawf): it must be based on overwhelming probability (ghalabat al-zann) from an observable sign, personal experience, or a trustworthy Muslim doctor's report. The text notes that for minor impurity (hadath) within the town, there is consensus that tayammum for cold is not allowed (because one can heat water or find a bathhouse).

Key Terms
البرد (al-bard): Severe cold that can cause harm or death if one uses water.
غلبة الظن (ghalabat al-zann): Overwhelming probability, the standard for determining fear of harm in illness or cold.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the Sahibayn's prohibition of tayammum due to cold inside the town?
A) They consider cold not a valid excuse.
B) They assume hot water is generally available in towns.
C) They require a doctor's note.
D) They follow a stricter definition of fear.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows tayammum for severe cold based on genuine fear, but the location and availability of hot water create an intra-school dispute between Imam and Sahibayn.

Conditions for Tayammum: Distance, Illness, Cold, and Fear
Explains conditions for tayammum with measurements and ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for tayammum and explain the intra-madhhab difference on cold in the city.

Core Idea
Tayammum is permitted for the resident who fears harm from water due to illness or cold, as long as the inability is established.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explores the conditions that allow a person to perform tayammum instead of wudu or ghusl. It covers the distance criterion, the case of illness, and the case of extreme cold. The Hanafi school adopts a broader approach than some others, permitting tayammum for any genuine harm, not just life-threatening situations. The text also records an internal disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding cold inside the city.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Tayammum is a substitute purification when water is unavailable or harmful. Its ruling depends on the situation: it can be wajib if water is absent for an obligatory act, or permissible if there is a valid excuse. The passage focuses on when it is permitted.
2
Understand the distance condition
Water is considered far if it is one mile (one-third of a farsakh) away. Alternative views: Muhammad says two miles absolutely; Abu Yusuf gives a practical criterion: if going for water causes the caravan to disappear from sight, it is far. The author considers the one-mile view strongest.
Measurements: 1 mile = 4000 cubits; 1 cubit = 24 finger-breadths.
3
Analyze the illness condition
For illness, fear of harm from using water (e.g., worsening the illness) permits tayammum. The Hanafi school permits absolutely, while al-Shafi'i restricts to fear of death or loss of limb. The passage also discusses the case of a patient who cannot purify at all: according to Sahibayn he delays prayer, but the fatwa is to imitate the movements and repeat later.
Harm includes intensification of illness, inability to move, or pain.
4
Examine the cold condition and the ikhtilaf
For extreme cold, if using water leads to fear of death or illness, tayammum is permitted. Abu Hanifa permits even inside the city, because the inability is real. Sahibayn restrict to outside the city, since hot water is typically available to residents. The text notes that the correct view is to prohibit it in the city by consensus (meaning the Sahibayn view prevails).
Fear is established by ghalabat al-zann (predominant presumption) from experience, a sign, or a reliable Muslim doctor.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
For distance, the mufta bihi is one mile as per the matn. For illness, the absolute Hanafi view is fatwa. For cold inside the city, the mufta bihi is the Sahibayn position (not permissible). The text states 'والأصح عدم جوازه للمحدث في المصر إجماعاً'.
The tarjih is based on later authorities such as al-Haskafi and Ibn Abidin.

(عيني، مستخلص) لمرض: أي بأن خاف اشتداده باستعمال الماء، أو بالتحرك للاستعمال، أو لم يقدر على استعماله، وعند الشافعي: يتيمم إذا خاف تلف النفس أو العضو، وعندنا يتيمم مطلقاً، أما إذا لم يقدر المريض على الوضوء ولا التيمم وليس عنده من يوضئه أو ييممه فإنه لا يصلي عندهما، وفي "الجامع الصغير" أن مقطوع اليدين والرجلين إن كان بوجهه جراحة يصلي بغير طهارة ولا يتيمم ولا يعيد، وفي "التنوير" وشرحه فاقد الطهورين والتراب - لمرض يؤخرها، وقالا يتشبه بالمصلين، فيركع ويسجد إن وجد مكاناً يابساً، وإلا يؤمي يعيد، وبه يفتى، وإليه صح رجوع الإمام. (فتح المعين) أو برد: يعني إذا كان الماء قائماً ثم خاف الجنب إن اغتسل أو توضأ أن يقتله البرد أو يمرضه يتيمم مطلقاً، سواء كان خارج المصر أو فيه، وعندهما لا فيه؛ لأن تيسر الماء الحار للمقيم في المصر غالب، وللإمام أن العجز قد ثبت في حقه حقيقة فيعتبر، والأصح عدم جوازه للمحدث في المصر إجماعاً، والمراد بالخوف في المرض والبرد هو غلبة الظن عن أمارة أو تجربة أو بإخبار طبيب مسلم غير ظاهر الفسق. (فتح وغيره)
For his being a mile away: [It is one-third of a farsakh, four thousand cubits, each cubit being twenty-four finger-breadths... and letters لا إله إلا الله محمد رسول الله (ع،ط)] Whether that is in a city or outside it. It was said regarding a traveler if water is in front of him: estimated at two miles. From Muhammad that it is estimated at two miles absolutely. It was said: if he is in a place where he can hear the sound of the people of water, it is close; otherwise far. From Abu Yusuf that if water is such that if he goes to it and performs wudu, the caravan goes away and disappears from sight, it is far; otherwise near. This is very good, and what the author mentioned is the closest of the opinions. (Ayni, Mustakhlas) For illness: meaning he fears its intensification by using water, or by moving to use it, or he is unable to use it. According to al-Shafi'i, he performs tayammum only if he fears loss of life or limb; according to us, he performs tayammum absolutely. But if the patient is unable to perform wudu or tayammum and there is no one to help him perform either, then according to the two, he does not pray. In al-Jami' al-Saghir: one whose hands and feet are cut off and has a wound on his face prays without purification and does not perform tayammum nor repeat. In al-Tanwir and its commentary: one who lacks both purifiers (water and earth) due to illness delays prayer. The two said: he imitates the worshippers, bowing and prostrating if he finds a dry place; otherwise gestures and repeats. This is the fatwa and to this the Imam's return is correct. (Fath al-Mu'in) For cold: meaning if water is present and a junub fears that if he bathes or performs wudu, the cold will kill him or make him ill, he performs tayammum absolutely, whether outside the city or inside. According to the two, not inside, because hot water is generally available for the resident in the city. For the Imam, the inability is established in his favor, so it is considered. The correct view is that it is not permissible for a hadath in the city by consensus. The meaning of fear in illness and cold is predominant presumption based on a sign, experience, or the report of a Muslim doctor who is not apparently sinful. (Fath and others)

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the conditions for tayammum. It starts with the condition of distance: a mile is defined as one-third of a farsakh (approx. 4,000 cubits). Various views are given: Muhammad al-Shaybani estimates two miles absolutely; Abu Yusuf gives a practical criterion (the caravan disappears from sight). The author notes that what the matn states (one mile) is the closest opinion. For illness, a Shafi'i opinion is given that tayammum is only allowed if there is fear of death or loss of limb, while the Hanafi position is absolute (any harm). There is a discussion of a patient unable to purify: according to Sahibayn, he does not pray; but the fatwa is to imitate the movements and repeat later. For cold, the same absolute permission is given according to Imam Abu Hanifa, while Sahibayn restrict it to outside the city because hot water is available to residents. The text adds that fear must be based on a reasonable presumption, not mere whim.

Key Terms
الفرسخ (al-farsakh): A unit of distance: one farsakh equals 12,000 cubits; one mile is one-third of a farsakh (4,000 cubits).
غلبة الظن (ghalabat al-zann): Predominant presumption, a degree of certainty that falls short of absolute certainty but suffices for practical legal obligations.

Ruling on Wiping Over the Overshoe (Jarmuq) and Its Conditions
Ruling on the overshoe (jarmuq) and related cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on wiping over the jarmuq, identify the conditions required, and explain the dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i over the 'substitute for a substitute' argument.

Core Idea
Wiping over the jarmuq is valid because it follows the khuff, but only if worn before hadath.

This lesson examines a practical issue for Muslims in cold climates: wearing an overshoe (jarmuq) over the leather sock (khuff). The Hanafi school permits wiping over the jarmuq under specific conditions, basing its ruling on the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) as reported by Umar. We will explore the conditions, the duration of wiping, and the key disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Determine the act and its ruling
The act is wiping over the jarmuq worn over the khuff. The ruling is valid (sahih) when conditions are met.
Wiping is a substitute for washing the feet.
2
Identify the conditions for validity
The jarmuq must be worn before breaking wudu. If there is fear of cold, the necessity removes the time limit, making wiping permissible indefinitely.
Conditions include wearing before hadath and the validity of the underlying khuff.
3
Analyze the inter-madhhab dispute
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that wiping over the jarmuq creates a substitute for a substitute. The Hanafi response cites the hadith of Umar and the principle of taba'iyyah (the jarmuq follows the khuff).
Both schools have valid proofs; the Hanafi position is supported by direct narration.
4
Apply the ruling to specific cases
If water enters the khuff and covers the entire foot, wiping is invalid. If most of the foot is intentionally removed from the khuff, wiping is invalid depending on the amount.
Intentional removal invalidates; unintentional removal due to looseness does not.
5
Note the implication for duration
A resident who wipes and then travels before completing a day and night changes to the traveler's period (three days). If he travels after completing the resident period, his period does not change.
The duration shift is agreed upon when wiping has begun.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكم الجرموق وغيره: وصح على الجرموق إن لم يخف ذهاب رجليه من البرد، وبعدهما غسل رجليه فقط، وخروج أكثر القدم أي نزع الخف ومضي المدة كدف أي من الخف نزع، ولو مسح مقيم فسافر قبل تمام يوم وليلةٍ مسح ثلاثا، ولو أقام مسافر بعد يوم وليلة نزع، وإلا يتم يوما وليلة. خفيه وغسل رجليه وصح على الجرموق، إن لم يخف إلخ: يعني إن انقضت مدة المسح وهو مسافر ويخاف ذهاب رجليه من البرد لو نزع خفيه جاز المسح عليهما من غير توقيت، فعلى هذا يستوعب الخف، ولو مسح عليهما ثم دخل الماء الخف، وأقبل جميع القدم وبلغ الماء الكعب بطل المسح، وروي عن أبي حنيفة أنه يجب عليه غسل الرجل الأخرى. (فتح، مسكين) من البرد لأن موضع الضرورة مستثنى عن أدلة الشرع، فالخف يصير كالجبيرة وهي غير مؤقتة، لكن يستوعب الخف بالمسح حينئذ. (مستخلص) غسل رجليه: يعني لا يعيد الوضوء خلافاً للشافعي. رمز الحقائق: ٢٩/١] فقط دون بقية أعضائه، وقال الشافعي: يعيد الوضوء. (ط) مسكين) نزع كنزع كله في الصحيح، وعن محمد: إن بقي من ظهر القدم في موضع المسح قدر ثلاث أصابع لم يبطل وهو الأصح وهو قول أبي يوسف، وعن أبي حنيفة، إن زال عقب الرجل أو زال أكثر عقبه بطل مسحه . وهذا إذا أخرجه قصداً حتى لو خرج لا عن قصد بأن كان واسعاً يرتفع العقب برفع الرجل إلى الساق، ويعود بالوضع جاز له المسح بالإجماع. (الدر المختار) تتحول ولو مسح مقيم إلخ: قيد بقوله: "مسح "؛ لأنه لو لبس وهو مقيم وسافر قبل أن تنتقض الطهارة، و مسح مدته إلى مدة السفر اتفاقاً، وقيد بقوله: قبل يوم وليلة؛ لأنه لو سافر بعد مضي مدة الإقامة لا تتحول مدته إلى مدة السفر بالاتفاق؛ لأن مانعية الخف قد زال والحدث قد سرى إلى القدمين (مسكين مستخلص) ثلاثاً : أي من الأيام والليالى، وقال الشافعي ومالك: إن سافر بعد ما مسح يتم يوماً وليلة لا غيره. (ع) وإلا يتم: أي وإن أقام قبل مسح يوم وليلة. على الجرموق الشامل على الخف ويقال له: موق أيضاً، وهو فارسي معرب خف كبير واسع يلبس فوق الخف، وقيدنا الموق بـ"الشامل على الخف"؛ لأنه لو لبس الجرموق وحده جاز المسح اتفاقاً، وإن لبسه فوق الخف يجوز المسح عليه عندنا، وقال الشافعي: لا يجوز المسح عليه؛ لأن المسح على الخف بدل عن الغسل، فلو جوزنا المسح على الجرموق لجعلنا للبدل بدلاً، وهذا لا يجوز، ولنا ما روي عن عمر الله أنه قال: رأيت رسول الله على الجرموقين؛ ولأنه تبع للخف، ولذا شاركه في حالة الانفراد والمسح على الجرموق فيما إذا لبسه قبل أن يحدث، أما إذا أحدث ومسح على الخف أو لم يمسح ثم لبس الجرموق لا يمسح عليه. (مستخلص، مسكين)
Ruling on the overshoe (jarmuq) and other things: It is valid to wipe over the jarmuq if he does not fear loss of his feet from cold; after them, he only washes his feet. Emergence of most of the foot means removing the khuff and passage of the period. And if a resident wiped and then traveled before completing a day and night, he wipes three (days and nights); if a traveler settles after a day and night he removes, otherwise he completes a day and night. He removes his khuffs and washes his feet. It is valid to wipe over the jarmuq. 'If he does not fear' etc. means: if the wiping period has expired while he is a traveler and he fears losing his feet from cold if he removes his khuffs, wiping over them is permitted without time limitation. On this basis, he wipes the entire khuff. If he wiped over them and then water entered the khuff, covering all of the foot and reaching the ankles, the wiping is invalid. It is narrated from Abu Hanifa that he must wash the other foot. (Fath, Miskeen) Due to cold, because the place of necessity is exempted from the evidences of Shari'ah, so the khuff becomes like a splint (jabirah) which is not time-limited. He wipes the entire khuff in that case. (Mustakhlas) 'He washes his feet' means he does not repeat wudu, contrary to al-Shafi'i. Rami al-Haqa'iq: 29/1] Only the feet, not the rest of his limbs. Al-Shafi'i said: he repeats wudu. (T, Miskeen) 'Removing' is like removing all in the correct view. From Muhammad: if there remains on the top of the foot at the place of wiping the size of three fingers, it does not invalidate, and this is the most correct, and it is the view of Abu Yusuf. From Abu Hanifa: if the heel of the foot is removed or most of it is removed, his wiping is invalid. This if he intentionally removed them. If he removed without intention, e.g., the khuff was loose and the heel rises when raising the foot to the shin and returns when placing, wiping is permissible by consensus. (al-Durr al-Mukhtar) It changes. 'If a resident wiped' etc. is restricted by his saying 'wiped'; because if he wore while resident and travelled before the purification is broken, his period changes to the traveler's period by agreement. Restricted by 'before a day and night' because if he travels after the resident period has passed, his period does not change to traveler's by agreement, because the preventing capacity of the khuff has ceased and the hadath has reached the feet. (Miskeen, Mustakhlas) 'Three' means days and nights. Al-Shafi'i and Malik said: if he travels after wiping, he completes the day and night, not more. (Ayni) 'Otherwise he completes' means if he settles before wiping a day and night. On the jarmuq that covers the khuff, also called 'muq', it is a Persianized word meaning a large loose khuff worn over the khuff. We restricted 'muq' to 'covering the khuff' because if he wears the jarmuq alone, wiping is permissible by agreement. If he wears it over the khuff, wiping over it is permissible according to us. Al-Shafi'i said: wiping over it is not permissible, because wiping over the khuff is a substitute for washing; if we allow wiping over the jarmuq, we are making a substitute for a substitute, and this is not allowed. Our proof is what was narrated from Umar (may Allah have mercy on him) that he said: I saw the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) wearing the jarmuqayn. And because it follows the khuff, therefore it shares with it in the case of being worn alone. Wiping over the jarmuq is when he wears it before breaking wudu. But if he commits hadath and wipes over the khuff or does not wipe then wears the jarmuq, he does not wipe over it. (Mustakhlas, Miskeen)
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that expands into an inter-madhhab dispute. It addresses the validity of wiping over an overshoe (jarmuq) worn over the khuff. The permissibility is established for necessity (cold). The text then discusses various cases: if the moaning of the khuff is removed, if water enters, the duration of wiping for a traveler who becomes resident, and the dispute with Shafi'i and Malik on whether the duration changes. The core Hanafi position is that wiping over the jarmuq is permissible when worn over the khuff, based on the hadith from Umar and because the jarmuq is a follower (tabi') of the khuff. The Shafi'i objection is that it creates a substitute-for-a-substitute, which is invalid. The Hanafi response is that the jarmuq is not a new substitute but a protective covering for the original khuff. The passage also covers the condition of intentional removal and the amount of the foot that must remain for wiping to be valid.

Key Terms
الجرموق (al-jarmuq): A large, loose overshoe worn over the khuff (leather sock) for protection, often in cold climates.
الجبيرة (al-jabirah): A splint or bandage placed on a wound. In this context, the khuff is treated like a jabirah when necessity dictates, meaning no time limit on wiping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Wiping over Jarmuq: Necessity, Conditions, and Intra-Madhhab Differences
Ruling on jarmuq with necessity condition and intra-madhhab differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which wiping over jarmuq is permissible and apply the Hanafi ruling for its nullification.

Core Idea
Wiping over jarmuq in necessity is unlimited in time, like a splint, but the entire khuff must be covered.

The Hanafi school permits wiping over jarmuq (overshoes) under specific conditions of necessity, such as a traveler fearing foot damage from cold after the normal wiping period expires. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentary clarifies the ruling, likening jarmuq to a splint (jabira) which has no time limit. It also addresses disputes within the school about how much of the foot must remain under the khuff for wiping to stay valid, and contrasts the Hanafi position with that of Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the effect of water entering the khuff.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine that wiping over jarmuq is a valid act under necessity. The base text states 'and it is valid to wipe over jarmuq'.
The ruling is wajib (obligatory) in necessity, not fard, due to the evidence being zanni.
2
Understand the necessity condition
The condition for unlimited wiping is that the traveler's normal wiping period (one day and night for resident, three for traveler) has expired, and removing the khuff would cause fear of foot damage from cold.
In this case, the jarmuq is treated like a splint (jabira) with no time limit, but the entire khuff must be wiped.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on partial removal
If part of the foot exits the khuff, Abu Hanifa holds that removing the heel or most of the heel invalidates wiping. Muhammad and Abu Yusuf hold that wiping remains valid if at least three fingers' width of the back of the foot remains.
The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that of Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, as it is considered al-asahh (most correct).
4
Compare with the Shafi'i position on water entry
If water enters the khuff and wets the foot to the ankle, the Hanafi school rules that the wiping is invalidated, but only the affected foot needs to be washed, not the entire wudu repeated. Imam al-Shafi'i requires a full new wudu.
The Hanafi view is based on the principle that nullification of wiping does not nullify the entire wudu.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكم الجرموق وغيره
إن لم يخف ذهاب رجليه من البرد، وبعدهما غسل رجليه فقط، وخروج أكثر القدم
أي الخرق
أي نزع الخف ومضي المدة ك دف
أي من الخف نزع، ولو مسح مقيم فسافر قبل تمام يوم وليلةٍ مسح ثلاثا، ولو أقام مسافر بعد يوم
وليلة نزع، وإلا يتم يوما وليلة.
خفيه وغسل رجليه
[حكم الجرموق وغيره] وصح على الجرموق،
مسح
إن لم يخف إلخ: يعني إن انقضت مدة المسح وهو مسافر ويخاف ذهاب رجليه من البرد لو نزع خفيه جاز المسح عليهما من غير توقيت، فعلى هذا يستوعب الخف، ولو مسح عليهما ثم دخل الماء الخف، وأقبل جميع القدم وبلغ الماء الكعب بطل المسح، وروي عن أبي حنيفة أنه يجب عليه غسل الرجل الأخرى. (فتح، مسكين) من البرد لأن موضع الضرورة مستثنى عن أدلة الشرع، فالخف يصير كالجبيرة وهي غير مؤقتة، لكن يستوعب الخف بالمسح حينئذ. (مستخلص) غسل رجليه: يعني لا يعيد الوضوء خلافاً للشافعي. رمز الحقائق: ٢٩/١] فقط دون بقية أعضائه، وقال الشافعي: يعيد الوضوء. (ط) مسكين) نزع كنزع كله في الصحيح، وعن محمد: إن بقي من ظهر القدم في موضع المسح قدر ثلاث أصابع لم يبطل وهو الأصح وهو قول أبي يوسف، وعن أبي حنيفة، إن زال عقب الرجل أو زال أكثر عقبه بطل مسحه . وهذا إذا أخرجه قصداً حتى لو خرج لا عن قصد بأن كان واسعاً يرتفع العقب برفع الرجل إلى الساق، ويعود بالوضع جاز له المسح بالإجماع. (الدر المختار)
Ruling on jarmuq and other: If he does not fear the loss of his feet from cold, and after them washing his feet only, and exit of most of the foot. Meaning the tear. Meaning removal of khuff and passage of time like such. Meaning removal from the khuff. And if a resident wiped and then traveled before completion of a day and night, he wipes three, and if a traveler settled after a day and night he removes, otherwise complete a day and night. His khuff and washing his feet. [Ruling on jarmuq and other] and it is valid to wipe over jarmuq. Wiping. 'If he does not fear' etc.: meaning if the period of wiping has expired and he is a traveler and fears his feet leaving from cold if he removes his khuff, wiping over them is permissible without time limit. So on this basis, he covers the entire khuff. And if he wiped over them then water entered the khuff and the entire foot came forward and water reached the ankle, the wiping is invalidated. It is narrated from Abu Hanifa that he must wash the other foot. (Fath, Miskin) from cold because the place of necessity is excepted from the evidences of Shariah, so the khuff becomes like a splint (jabira), which is not time-limited, but he covers the khuff with wiping at that time. (Mustakhlas) 'Washing his feet': meaning he does not repeat wudu contrary to Shafi'i. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 29/1) only without the rest of his limbs, and Shafi'i said: he repeats wudu. (T, Miskin) Removal is like removing all in the correct view. From Muhammad: if a portion of the back of the foot remains in the place of wiping to the extent of three fingers it does not invalidate, and this is the most correct and it is the view of Abu Yusuf. From Abu Hanifa: if the heel of the foot is removed or most of the heel is removed, his wiping is invalidated. This is when he removes it intentionally. Even if it comes out unintentionally, e.g. it was wide, the heel rises when lifting the foot to the shin, and returns when placing, wiping is permissible by consensus. (al-Durr al-Mukhtar)
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on wiping over jarmuq (overshoes). The base text from Kanz al-Daqa'iq states that wiping over jarmuq is valid, then the commentary clarifies conditions. The key principle: if a traveler's wiping period has expired and he fears foot damage from cold due to removing the khuff, he may continue wiping over the jarmuq without time limit, likening it to a splint (jabira) which is not time-bound. The commentator notes that in this case, one must cover the entire khuff with wiping. If water enters the khuff and wets the foot to the ankle, the wiping is nullified. There is a narration from Abu Hanifa that he must then wash only that foot, not repeat the whole wudu, contrary to Shafi'i. Regarding partial removal of the foot: Muhammad and Abu Yusuf say wiping remains valid if at least three fingers' width of the back foot remains; Abu Hanifa says removing the heel or most of it invalidates wiping. This applies only if done intentionally; if unintentional due to a wide khuff, wiping is valid by consensus.

Key Terms
جَرْمُوْق (jarmūq): A wide overshoe worn over the khuff to protect from cold; also called mūq.
نَزْع (naz‘): Removal of the khuff from the foot, which nullifies the wiping.
جَبِيْرَة (jabīrah): A splint used for a broken bone; analogically, wet wiping over it is not time-limited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a traveler's wiping period ends but he fears foot damage from cold, how should he wipe over his jarmuq?
A) He must remove the jarmuq and wash his feet.
B) He may continue wiping over the jarmuq without time limit, covering the entire khuff.
C) He must wipe for only one day and night.
D) He must repeat wudu entirely.

Key Takeaway
In necessity, jarmuq wiping is like a splint – no time limit, but full coverage required.

Change of Wiping Duration with Change of Travel Status
Ruling on conversion of wiping duration when travel status changes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a resident's wiping duration converts to a traveler's duration and identify the key condition that triggers conversion.

Core Idea
The wiping duration changes only if the travel status changes before the original period expires.

Wiping over khuff has different durations for residents (one day) and travelers (three days). But what happens if your status changes mid-wiping? This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains the precise condition for the duration to convert. It also highlights a disagreement with the Shafi'i and Maliki schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The passage rules on conversion of wiping duration when a resident travels after wiping, or a traveler settles.
The act is wiping over khuff.
2
Note the condition for conversion
Conversion occurs only if the change in status (travel or residence) happens before the original period (one day for resident, three for traveler) is completed.
If travel occurs after the resident period ends, no conversion.
3
Understand the consensus
There is consensus that if travel occurs after the resident period has passed, the duration does not convert. The khuff's preventative quality ceases.
4
Contrast with other schools
Shafi'i and Maliki schools hold that the initial intention determines the duration; no conversion occurs at all.
This leads to practical differences.
5
Apply to a scenario
Given a specific timeline, determine whether conversion applies and calculate the remaining wiping duration.
Practice with real examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو مسح مقيم إلخ : قيد بقوله: "مسح "؛ لأنه لو لبس وهو مقيم وسافر قبل أن تنتقض الطهارة، و مسح مدته إلى مدة السفر اتفاقاً، وقيد بقوله: قبل يوم وليلة؛ لأنه لو سافر بعد مضي مدة الإقامة لا تتحول مدته إلى مدة السفر بالاتفاق؛ لأن مانعية الخف قد زال والحدث قد سرى إلى القدمين (مسكين مستخلص) ثلاثاً : أي من الأيام والليالى، وقال الشافعي ومالك: إن سافر بعد ما مسح يتم يوماً وليلة لا غيره. (ع) وإلا يتم: أي وإن أقام قبل مسح يوم وليلة.
And if a resident wiped etc.: He restricted by his saying 'wiped' because if he wore while resident and traveled before the purity was broken, his duration converts to the duration of travel by agreement. And restricted by his saying 'before a day and night' because if he traveled after the residence period had passed, his duration does not convert to the travel duration by agreement; because the preventative quality of the khuff has ceased and the hadath has reached the feet. (Miskin, Mustakhlas) 'three': i.e., days and nights. And Shafi'i and Malik said: if he traveled after he wiped, he completes one day and night, nothing else. (A) 'otherwise complete': i.e., if he settles before wiping a day and night.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on how the wiping duration changes when a person's travel status changes. The key condition is that the change (from resident to traveler or vice versa) must occur before the original duration is complete. If a resident wears khuff and then travels before his wudu breaks, his wiping duration extends to that of a traveler (three days and nights) by consensus. However, if he travels after the resident period (one day and night) has passed, the duration does not convert; the khuff's preventative effect is gone and the hadath has taken effect. Similarly, if a traveler settles before completing one day and night of wiping, he must remove his khuff and wash his feet. The passage contrasts the Hanafi position with Shafi'i and Malik, who hold that the traveler's duration is fixed regardless of subsequent travel.

Key Terms
مُقِيْم (muqīm): Resident, not traveling; opposite of musafir.
انْتِقَاض الطَّهَارَة (intiqāḍ al-ṭahārah): The breaking of wudu; a condition for the validity of wiping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a resident wears khuff and wipes, then travels after one full day and night, according to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling?
A) He continues wiping for three days from the start.
B) He must remove the khuff and wash his feet, as the duration is complete.
C) He wipes for one more day.
D) His duration does not convert and he must remove immediately.

Key Takeaway
The wiping duration converts only if the change in travel status occurs before the original period ends; otherwise, the duration is complete and the khuff must be removed.

Wiping over Jarmuq: The Overshoe Over the Khuff
Ruling on wiping over jarmuq worn over khuff with Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wiping over a jarmuq worn over a khuff, identify the Shafi'i counterargument, and apply the condition that the jarmuq must be worn before hadath.

Core Idea
Wiping over a jarmuq is valid only if worn before hadath; it follows the khuff's ruling.

This passage addresses a practical scenario: a person wears a large overshoe (jarmuq or mūq) over their leather khuff. The Hanafi school permits wiping over the jarmuq under one condition, while the Shafi'i school rejects it entirely. The dispute hinges on whether the jarmuq is a 'substitute for a substitute' or a 'follower' of the khuff. We will examine the proofs and reach the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling in Hanafi school
The act is wiping over a jarmuq (overshoe) that covers a khuff. The Hanafi ruling: it is permissible (jā’iz) provided the jarmuq is worn before the occurrence of hadath (ritual impurity).
This ruling applies only when the jarmuq covers the khuff; if worn alone, it is permissible by consensus.
2
Understand the Shafi'i objection
Shafi'i argues that wiping over the khuff is itself a substitute (badal) for washing the feet. Allowing wiping over the jarmuq would make it a substitute for a substitute, which is invalid in their usul.
The key principle is 'no substitute for a substitute' (lā badal ‘alā badal).
3
Examine the Hanafi proofs
Hanafi relies on the hadith of ‘Umar: 'I saw the Messenger of Allah ﷺ wearing jarmūqayn.' This indicates permissibility. Additionally, the jarmuq is a follower (taba‘) of the khuff, sharing its rulings when worn alone, so it does not become an independent substitute.
The hadith provides direct textual evidence, and the concept of taba‘iyya avoids the Shafi'i objection.
4
Apply the condition
The jarmuq must be worn while the person is in a state of purity (wudu). If one already has hadath and then puts on the jarmuq, wiping over it is not allowed. Similarly, if one had wiped over the khuff and then put on the jarmuq, wiping is not allowed.
This condition is derived from the analogy with the khuff itself, which must be put on in a state of purity.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Hanafi: person can wipe over the jarmuq without removing it. Shafi'i: must remove the jarmuq and wipe directly on the khuff. This affects convenience, especially in cold weather or for travelers.
The Hanafi view is mufta bihi (relied upon) in the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على الجرموق الشامل على الخف ويقال له: موق أيضاً، وهو فارسي معرب خف كبير واسع يلبس فوق الخف، وقيدنا الموق بـ"الشامل على الخف"؛ لأنه لو لبس الجرموق وحده جاز المسح اتفاقاً، وإن لبسه فوق الخف يجوز المسح عليه عندنا، وقال الشافعي: لا يجوز المسح عليه؛ لأن المسح على الخف بدل عن الغسل، فلو جوزنا المسح على الجرموق لجعلنا للبدل بدلاً، وهذا لا يجوز ، ولنا ما روي عن عمر الله أنه قال: رأيت رسول الله على الجرموقين؛ ولأنه تبع للخف، ولذا شاركه في حالة الانفراد والمسح على الجرموق فيما إذا لبسه قبل أن يحدث، أما إذا أحدث ومسح على الخف أو لم يمسح ثم لبس الجرموق لا يمسح عليه. (مستخلص، مسكين) مسح
Over the jarmuq that covers the khuff - it is also called mūq, and it is a Persian word borrowed into Arabic, a large wide khuff worn over the khuff. We restricted mūq to 'that covers the khuff' because if one wears the jarmuq alone, wiping is permissible by consensus. If he wears it over the khuff, wiping over it is permissible in our school. Shafi'i said: wiping over it is not permissible because wiping over the khuff is a substitute for washing. If we permit wiping over the jarmuq, we would be making a substitute for a substitute, which is not permissible. Our proof is what is narrated from 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said: I saw the Messenger of Allah ﷺ wearing jarmūqayn. And because it is a follower of the khuff, so it shares with it in the state of being alone. And wiping over the jarmuq applies when he wears it before he hadath. But if he had hadath and wiped over the khuff or did not wipe, then wore the jarmuq, he does not wipe over it. (Mustakhlas, Miskin) Wipe.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute between Hanafi and Shafi'i regarding wiping over a jarmuq (overshoe) that is worn over a khuff. The Hanafi school permits it, while Shafi'i prohibits it on the basis that it would be making a substitute for a substitute (the khuff itself being a substitute for washing the feet). The Hanafi school responds with two proofs: first, a hadith from 'Umar that the Prophet ﷺ wore jarmuqayn, which implies his wiping over them. Second, the jarmuq is a follower (tabi‘) of the khuff, since it shares in the ruling when worn alone; thus it is as if it were part of the khuff. However, there is a condition: the jarmuq must be worn before the hadath occurs. If one has hadath and wipes over the khuff (or does not wipe) and then puts on the jarmuq, wiping over the jarmuq is not allowed.

Key Terms
مُوْق (mūq): A large wide overshoe worn over the khuff; also called jarmuq.
تَبَع (taba‘): A follower; here it means the jarmuq follows the khuff in rulings.
بَدَل (badal): Substitute; here refers to wiping over the khuff as a substitute for washing the feet.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the condition for wiping over a jarmuq worn over a khuff?
A) The jarmuq must be worn before hadath occurs.
B) The jarmuq must be worn after wiping the khuff.
C) The jarmuq must be worn only during travel.
D) The jarmuq must be made of leather.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi school permits wiping over a jarmuq worn over a khuff only if it is worn before hadath; Shafi'i rejects it based on the 'no substitute for a substitute' principle.

Preventers and Nullifiers of Wiping over Khuff
Conditions and nullifiers of wiping over khuff

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the preventers and nullifiers of wiping over khuff according to the Hanafi school and apply the rules on tears to determine validity.

Core Idea
A large open tear of three fingers prevents wiping; nullifiers are wudu-breaker, removal, and time expiry.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains the conditions that prevent wiping over khuffs and the actions that nullify it once valid. Understanding these rules ensures your wiping is correct and avoids common mistakes, especially regarding how tears are measured and combined.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sunna method of wiping
The passage describes the recommended way: place fingers on the front of the khuff and draw to the shin, separating palms. This is the sunna; starting from the shin is valid but leaves the sunna.
The obligatory minimum is wiping any part of the khuff with wet hand.
2
Define the large tear (al-kharq al-kabir) as a preventer
A tear prevents wiping if it is open, revealing what is beneath, and measures three fingers of the foot, using the smallest spread fingers. Small tears do not prevent.
Tears in one khuff combine to reach the threshold; in two khuffs they do not.
3
Note the intra-madhhab and inter-madhhab differences
Zufar and al-Shafi'i hold any tear prevents; Malik holds even large does not. The Hanafi position is that only an open three-finger tear prevents. The text gives the tarjih for tears visible only while walking.
The relied-upon view is the most correct (al-asahh).
4
Enumerate the three nullifiers of wiping
Three things nullify: (1) anything that breaks wudu, (2) removal of the khuff (even one), (3) expiry of the time period (three days for traveller, one for resident). The actual nullifier is the previous hadath becoming effective upon removal or expiry.
Removal of one khuff nullifies the wiping for both feet because the original wudu substitute is lost.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موانع المسح ونواقضه
يبدأ من الأصابع إلى الساق.
[موانع المسح ونواقضه]
والخرق الكبير يمنعه، وهو قدر ثلاث أصابع القدم أصغرها، والقليل لا، ويجمع في خُفّ المسح وهو ما دون الثلاث لافيهما، بخلاف النّجاسة والانكشاف، وينقضه ناقض الوضوء ونزع خف ومضي المدة
وهو على وضوئه
من الأصابع: أي يضع أصابع يده اليمنى على مقدم خفه الأيمن ويضع أصابع يده اليسرى على مقدم خفه الأيسر ويمدهما إلى أصل الساق، هكذا روى المغيرة بن شعبة عن فعل رسول الله ، وسئل محمد الله عن المسح على الخفين فقال: أن يضع أصابع يديه على مقدم خفيه ويجافي كفيه ويمدهما إلى الساق ولو بدأ من قبل الساق يجوز إلا أنه ترك السنة، ولو مسح برؤوس الأصابع وجافى أصول الأصابع والكف لا يجوز، إلا أن يبتل مقدار الواجب. (مسكين) والخرق الكبير: [في أي جانب إذا كان منفرجا يرى ما تحته] وقال زفر والشافعي: يمنعه القليل أيضاً، وقال مالك: لا يمنعه الكبير أيضاً، ثم الخرق الكبير إنما يمنع جواز المسح إذا كان منفرجاً يرى ما تحته، فأما إذا كان لا يرى ما تحته بأن كان صلباً، لكنه يدخل فيه ثلاثة أصابع لا يمنع جواز المسح، وإن كان يبدو قدر ثلاثة أصابع حالة المشي لا في حالة وضع القدم على الأرض، قال بعضهم: يمنع وقال بعضهم: لا يمنع، وهو الأصح. (مسكين ملخصاً) أصغرها: فلو فرق الأصابع كانت هي المعتبرة، والصغير والكبير سواء. في خف: يعني لو كان الخرق في مواضع وكل موضع قدر إصبع أو أقل، وبالجميع يصير قدر ثلاثة أصابع، إن كان في خف واحد يجمع ويمنع المسح، وإن كان في خفين لا يجمع ولا يمنع وكذا لو كان الخرق على الساق لا يمنع جواز المسح، وإن كان أكثر من ثلاثة أصابع. (مسكين) بخلاف النجاسة: المتفرقة في خفيه أو في ثوبه أو بدنه أو مكانه أو في المجموع حيث تجمع. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٩/١] والانكشاف أي وبخلاف انكشاف العورة لو كانت متفرقة، والجمع يبلغ ربع أدنى عضو من يمنع الصلاة. (ط) ونزع خف: أي وينقضه أيضاً نزع الخف؛ لأن الخف مانع لسراية الحدث إلى القدم، فإذا زال المانع عاد الحدث السابق، وعمل عمله، وإفراد الخف ليعلم أن نزع خف واحد يكفي لانتقاض المسح؛ لتعذر الجمع بين الأصل والخلف في وظيفة واحدة. (مستخلص)
المنكشفة
الأعضاء
مضي المدة: أي وينقضه مضي المدة؛ لحديث صفوان بن عمار أن النبي عليه كان يأمرنا أن لا خفافنا تنزع ثلاثة أيام، والحديث أوجب كون الخف مانع سراية الحدث للرجل في المدة، فإذا مضى المدة عمل الحدث السابق عمله، ثم اعلم أن نزع الخف ومضي المدة غير ناقض في الحقيقة، إنما الناقض الحدث السابق، لكن الحدث يظهر عند النزع والمضي، فأضيف النقض إليهما. (مستخلص وغيره)
Preventers of wiping and its nullifiers. He begins from the toes to the shin. [Preventers and nullifiers of wiping] The large tear prevents it, and it is the extent of three toes of the foot, the smallest of them, and the small does not prevent. And they are combined in the khuff for wiping, i.e., what is less than three, not in both. Contrary to impurity (najasa) and exposure (inkishaf). And it is nullified by a nullifier of wudu, removal of the khuff, and passage of the time. And he is upon his wudu. 'From the toes': i.e., he places the fingers of his right hand on the front of his right khuff and the fingers of his left hand on the front of his left khuff and draws them to the base of the shin. Thus narrated al-Mughira ibn Shu'ba from the action of the Messenger of Allah . And Muhammad (al-Shaybani) was asked about wiping over the two khuffs and said: he places the fingers of his hands on the front of his khuffs and separates his palms and draws them to the shin. If he begins from the shin side, it is valid but he left the sunna. If he wiped with the tips of the fingers and separated the bases of the fingers and the palm, it is not valid unless the amount of the obligatory becomes wet. (Miskin) The large tear: in any side if it is open and what is beneath is visible. Zufar and Shafi'i said: the small also prevents. Malik said: the large also does not prevent. Then the large tear only prevents the validity of wiping if it is open such that what is beneath is visible. But if what is beneath is not visible, i.e., it is stiff, but three fingers can enter it, it does not prevent. If it appears to the extent of three fingers while walking, but not when the foot is placed on the ground, some said it prevents, some said it does not, and it is the most correct. (Miskin summarized) 'The smallest of them': if he spreads the fingers, those are what is considered; small and large are the same. 'In the khuff': meaning if the tear is in places, each place the size of a finger or less, and together they become the size of three fingers. If it is in one khuff, they are combined and prevent wiping. If in two khuffs, they are not combined and do not prevent. Similarly, if the tear is on the shin, it does not prevent the validity of wiping even if more than three fingers. (Miskin) Contrary to impurity: scattered on his two khuffs or his garment or his body or his place or in the total, where they are combined. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 29/1) And exposure: i.e., contrary to exposure of the 'awra if it is scattered, and the combination reaches a quarter of the smallest member that prevents prayer. (T) And removal of the khuff: i.e., it also nullifies, removal of the khuff; because the khuff prevents the transmission of hadath to the foot. When the preventer is removed, the previous hadath returns and acts. 'Khuff' is specified to teach that removal of one khuff suffices to nullify the wiping; because combining the original and the substitute in one function is impossible. (Mustakhlas) The exposed members. Passage of time: i.e., it nullifies, passage of time; due to the hadith of Safwan ibn 'Ammar that the Prophet used to command us not to remove our khuffs for three days. The hadith necessitated that the khuff prevents transmission of hadath to the foot for the period. When the period passes, the previous hadath acts. Then know that removal of the khuff and passage of time are not nullifiers in reality; rather the nullifier is the previous hadath, but the hadath manifests at the time of removal and passage, so nullification is attributed to them. (Mustakhlas and others)
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage covers the preventers (mawaani') and nullifiers (nawaqid) of wiping over khuffs. It first describes the sunna method of wiping: start from the toes and draw the hands to the shin, with fingers placed on the front and palms separated. Then it discusses tears: a large tear (three fingers' width) prevents wiping if it is open and reveals what is beneath. Small tears do not prevent. If multiple tears in one khuff are together three fingers, they prevent; if in two khuffs, they are not combined. Impurity and exposure of 'awra are treated differently (they combine even if scattered). For nullifiers, three things nullify wiping: (1) anything that nullifies wudu, (2) removal of the khuff (even one), and (3) expiration of the time period. The commentary explains that in reality, the nullifier is the previous hadath that becomes effective upon removal or time expiry, but it is attributed to these actions.

Key Terms
خَرْق (kharq): A tear or hole in the khuff that may expose the foot.
نَاقِض (nāqiḍ): That which nullifies the validity of wiping over the khuff.
مَانِع (māni‘): A condition that prevents the permissibility of wiping from the start.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following nullifies wiping over khuff according to the Hanafi school?
A) A small tear of one finger.
B) Removal of one khuff.
C) Walking with the khuff.
D) Wiping with dry hand.

Key Takeaway
Wiping over khuff is valid only when the khuff is intact (no large open tear) and is nullified by anything that breaks wudu, removal of the khuff, or expiry of the three-day/one-day period.

Duration of Wiping: Start Time and Length
States the duration for resident and traveler, starting from time of hadath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the duration of wiping over socks, identify the starting point as the time of the first hadath after wearing, and explain the Hanafi proof against the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
The wiping period begins from the first hadath after wearing, not from wearing or wiping.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq specifies the permitted duration for wiping over leather socks: one day and night for a resident, three days and nights for a traveler. The critical point is when that count starts. The Hanafi school uniquely anchors it to the moment of the first hadath (minor impurity) after putting on the socks, based on the sock's function as a barrier. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the opposing views of al-Shafi'i and Malik, and the evidence that supports the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Locate the basic rule: resident wipes for one day/night, traveler for three. Note that the count starts 'from the time of hadath' (مِنْ وَقْتِ الحَدَثِ).
The text explicitly says 'وَقْت الحَدَث يُوْماً وَلَيْلَةً لِلْمُقِيْمِ وَلِلْمُسَافِرِ ثَلَاثاً'.
2
Determine the Hanafi starting point
Understand that 'from the time of hadath' means the period begins when the first minor impurity occurs after wearing the socks – not from the moment of wearing nor from the first wipe.
The commentator clarifies that the intended meaning is 'before the hadath' but the period applies to the state after hadath occurs.
3
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the count starts from the time of the first wipe (مِن وَقْتِ المَسْحِ). Understand his reasoning: the limitation (taqdir) is for the sake of the wipe, so it should begin when wiping is done.
The text states: 'وَقَالَ الشَّافِعِي: ابْتِدَاءُ المُدَّةِ مِنْ وَقْتِ المَسْحِ'.
4
Follow the Hanafi proof and response
The Hanafi school argues that the sock (khuff) is recognized as a barrier (manʿ) preventing the transmission of hadath to the feet. Therefore, the count must start when that prevention becomes operative, i.e., when the hadath occurs. Also, the hadith of Safwan is cited as evidence.
الخُفّ عُرِفَ مَانِعاً مِن سَرَيَانِ الحَدَثِ بِالقَدَمِ، فَيُعْتَبَرُ مِنْ وَقْتِ المَنْعِ.
5
Apply the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Consider a concrete scenario: a resident performs wudu at dawn (Fajr), wears socks at sunrise, and has hadath after Asr. According to Hanafi, the next day he can pray Zuhr with wiping (24h from hadath is after Asr to next day Asr? Actually from hadath after Asr to next day Zuhr is <24h, so valid for Zuhr but not for Asr). According to Shafi'i, the period starts from the first wipe (which would be at the next wudu, e.g., before Fajr? Actually he wipes at dawn? The scenario is complex. The text gives: he performs wudu at dawn, wears at sunrise, has hadath after Asr. According to Hanafi, he can wipe for Zuhr the next day; according to the opposing view (from wearing), he cannot.
The text says: عِنْدَ الثَّلَاثَة: مِنْ وَقْتِ اللُّبْسِ... يُصَلِّي الظُّهْرَ مِنَ الغَدِ بِالمَسْحِ لَا العَصْرَ، وَعِنْدَهُمْ لَا يُصَلِّي الظُّهْرَ أَيْضاً.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقت الحدث يوماً وليلة للمقيم، وللمسافر ثلاثاً من وقت الحدث، أي صح المسح في يوم وليلة واحدة. وقت الحدث متعلق بقوله : وضوء تام وفيه توسع والمراد قبيل الحدث لا متصل به؛ لأن وقت الحدث لا يجامع الطهارة فكيف يكون ظرفا له مسكين) ووقت اللبس عند الشافعي، حتى لو غسل رجليه ولبس خفيه، ثم أتم الوضوء قبل أن يحدث جاز له المسح، وكذا لو لبسهما محدثاً، وخاض الماء حتى دخل الماء فيهما، ثم أتم سائر الأعضاء ثم أحدث جاز له أيضاً خلافاً له فيهما. [رمز الحقائق: ۲۸/۱] للمقيم إلخ: وعند مالك: لا يمسح المقيم أصلا ويمسح المسافر مؤبداً، واحتج بحديث عمار بن ياسر رضي الله عنه أنه قال: قلت: يا رسول الله ! أمسح على الخفين يوماً وليلة؟ قال: "نعم"، قلت: يومين؟ قال: "نعم"، حتى انتهيت إلى سبعة أيام، فقال علي: "إذا كنت في سفر فامسح ما بدالك"، وتأويله عندنا أن مراده عليه السلام أن المسح مؤبد غير منسوخ، وليس المراد أن لا ينزع الخف في هذه المدة، والأخبار المشهورة لا تترك بهذا الشاذ، فقد روي عن صفوان أتيت النبي ﷺ فقال : "ما جاء بك"؟ إلى أن قال : "إذا جئت فاسأل"، قال: فسألته عن المسح على الخفين، فقال عليه السلام: "للمقيم يوماً وليلة، وللمسافر ثلاثة أيام ولياليها". (مستخلص) من وقت الحدث: أي ابتداء المدة يعتبر من وقت الحدث الذي يوجد بعد اللبس وعند الثلاثة: من وقت اللبس من وقت المسح حتى لو توضأ المقيم عند طلوع الفجر ولبس عند طلوع الشمس وأحدث بعد ما صلى العصر، يصلي الظهر من الغد بالمسح لا العصر، وعندهم لا يصلي الظهر أيضاً بالمسح. [رمز الحقائق: ۲۸/۱] وقال الشافعي: ابتداء المدة من وقت المسح؛ لأن التقدير من أجله، فيعتبر من وقته، ولنا: أن الخف عرف مانعاً من سراية الحدث بالقدم، فيعتبر من وقت المنع. (عيني، فتح المعين)
The time of hadath: a day and a night for the resident, and for the traveler three days, from the time of the hadath. That is, wiping is valid for one day and one night. The phrase 'time of hadath' is attached to his statement 'complete wudu' and there is elaboration in it; the intended meaning is before the hadath, not contiguous with it, because the time of hadath does not coexist with purity, so how can it be a container for it? (Miskin) And the time of wearing (al-lubs) according to al-Shafi'i, so that if he washed his feet and put on his socks, then completed the wudu before having an hadath, wiping is permissible for him. Similarly, if he put them on while having hadath, and then entered water until water entered them, then completed the remaining limbs, then had an hadath, it is also permissible for him, contrary to al-Shafi'i in both. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 28/1] 'For the resident' etc.: According to Malik, the resident does not wipe at all, and the traveler wipes perpetually. He argued with the hadith of Ammar ibn Yasir (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said: I said, O Messenger of Allah, should I wipe over socks for a day and a night? He said: Yes. I said: Two days? He said: Yes, until I reached seven days. Then Ali said: 'If you are in a journey, wipe as long as you wish.' Our interpretation is that the Prophet's intended meaning is that wiping is perpetually valid and not abrogated, not that the sock is not to be removed during this period. And the well-known reports are not abandoned for this isolated one. It is reported from Safwan: I came to the Prophet ﷺ and he said: 'What brought you?' until he said: 'When you come, ask.' He said: So I asked him about wiping over socks, and he said: 'For the resident, a day and a night; for the traveler, three days and their nights.' (Mustakhlas) 'From the time of hadath': i.e., the beginning of the period is considered from the time of the hadath that occurs after wearing. According to the three (i.e., Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i? Actually 'the three' here refers to the other three imams: Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i? Wait, the text says 'inda al-thalathah' which usually means the three imams besides Abu Hanifa, i.e., Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, al-Shafi'i? But from context, it seems to be a dispute about starting point. The text says: 'according to the three, it is from the time of wearing or from the time of wiping.' So if a resident performed wudu at dawn, wore socks at sunrise, and had an hadath after praying Asr, he would pray Zuhr of the next day with wiping but not Asr. According to them, he would not pray Zuhr either with wiping. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 28/1] And al-Shafi'i said: the beginning of the period is from the time of wiping, because the limitation is for its sake, so it is considered from its time. Our evidence is: the sock is recognized as a barrier preventing the transmission of hadath to the foot, so it is considered from the time of prevention. (Ayni, Fath al-Muin)
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage discusses the duration of the concession for wiping over leather socks. The ruling is clear: a resident wipes for one day and one night (24 hours), and a traveler for three days and three nights (72 hours). The key point is the starting point: according to Abu Hanifa (and the Hanafi school), the count begins from the time of the first hadath (minor impurity) that occurs after wearing the socks. So if you put on socks after wudu, then later have an hadath, at that moment the clock starts ticking. This is based on the reasoning that the sock acts as a barrier preventing impurity from reaching the foot; the effect of this barrier becomes relevant only when there is something to prevent (i.e., hadath). The passage contrasts this with the position of Imam al-Shafi'i, who considers the count from the time of first wiping (i.e., when you actually wipe over the socks during wudu), and also with Imam Malik who forbids wiping for the resident altogether and allows it perpetually for the traveler. The Hanafi school provides a hadith from Safwan as evidence, and interprets the hadith of Ammar ibn Yasir in a way that does not contradict. The commentator also mentions a case: if a resident completes wudu at dawn, wears socks at sunrise, then has an hadath after Asr, according to the Hanafi position (from time of hadath), the next day he can pray Zuhr with wiping because the time from hadath (after Asr) to next day's Zuhr is less than 24 hours, but he cannot pray Asr because that would be beyond the period. According to the opposing view (from time of wearing), the period would have expired earlier. The Hanafi reasoning is reinforced by the principle that the sock's preventive function (man') is the basis, so the time should relate to when the prevention becomes operative.

Key Terms
وقت الحدث (waqt al-hadath): The moment when a minor impurity (hadath) occurs after wearing the socks, which marks the start of the permissible wiping period.
خف (khuff): Leather sock or boot that covers the foot and ankle, over which wiping (mash) is permissible under certain conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) From the time the socks are put on
B) From the time of the first hadath after wearing
C) From the time of the first wipe
D) From the time of wudu completion

Key Takeaway
The wiping period starts from the first hadath after wearing, not from wearing or wiping.
